### I need a new compiler...

- **From:** "Paul H" <NoSpamphobergNoSpam@att.net>
- **Date:** 2008-12-10T22:22:57-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<494095a5$0$5502$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net>`

```
I've been using MicroFocus Net Express for many years, but now I want to 
write a small application that would sell for maybe $20.00.  MF would charge 
a run-time fee to each user, so I need to select a different Compiler. 
Where can I find a list of inexpensive compilers, with ratings?  Does such a 
list exist?  TIA, Paul
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: I need a new compiler...

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-12-12T00:53:26+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6qcd9oFbr6ltU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<494095a5$0$5502$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <49409903$0$5487$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net>`

```
Paul H wrote:
> I've been using MicroFocus Net Express for many years, but now I want
> to write a small application that would sell for maybe $20.00.  MF
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> Thanks.

Paul,

given the criteria, have you thought about using Open COBOL?

(The price is pretty right)

One reason I moved to Fujitsu was because of MicroFocus Runtime Fees. (The 
main reason was because they shafted the VISOC user base... but that's a 
long time ago and can fairly be considered water under the bridge.)

Later of course, I moved off COBOL altogether and today enjoy a free 
compiler, a free IDE that is light years ahead of the COBOL one, and instant 
support directly from the Internet for free.

Nevertheless, if you really don't want to change languages, Open COBOL might 
be an option worth investigating.

There are more existing lines of code written for Open Source now, than 
there are for COBOL in the World. (Some of the Open Source may be written in 
COBOL, but it would be an insignificant amount.)

Even though the Open Source movement has failed to really penetrate the 
commercial marketplace to any significant extent (so far...) it is well 
worth keeping an eye on what they are doing.

I like their approach; most of what they are writing is components and they 
are getting a very good uptake for them.

Here's what they claim: 
http://www.eweek.com/index2.php?option=content&task=view&id=50786&pop=1&hide_ads=1&page=0&hide_js=1

I have been saying for a long time that the age of source code being King is 
gone. People are using Open Source binaries without requiring the source and 
most open "source" is NOT source at all.

Although I have bought and paid for the (old) fujitsu NetCOBOL and 
PowerCOBOL compilers (Not the (new) .NETCOBOL for .NET CLR generating) 
compiler, if I didn't have those compilers available and REALLY needed to do 
something in COBOL, I'd DEFINITELY have a look at Open COBOL.

Maybe, if it is a small project, it might be time to consider moving to C#? 
You can download the compiler, the IDE, and video tutorials on how to use 
them, all for free. The community is very supportive and I've never had a 
problem that wasn't solved, usually in minutes.

Or maybe Java? You can get Eclipse for free and Java is free. Both excellent 
and useful alternatives to COBOL

Whatever you decide, I wish you luck in your enterprise.

Pete.
```

```
On Thu, 11 Dec 2008 10:04:02 -0600, "Paul H" <NoSpamphobergNoSpam@att.net> wrote:

>Thanks Pete,
>But your alternatives mostly intimidate me.  Isn't using C or Java like 
>coding in assembly language? 

No, C is like Cobol, not assembly language. People who say C is like assembly language are
the ones who were frustrated by pointers. But Pete said C#, which is like Java. Both are
OO, and slightly higher level of abstraction than Cobol.

> If I stay with COBOL, none of the alternatives 
>offer the debugging environment like MF's.  Animating, breakpoints, value 
>monitoring, etc.  These make me productive. 

MF's anim command line debugger is crude compared to gdb. MF's NetExpress GUI debugger
isn't as good as Microsoft's various workbenches such as DevStudio formerly VisDev. 

> What do you think about Clarion?  

It's not a mainstream language, has roots in 4GL code generation.  

--- end of reply ---

>use it.  Maybe I'll give it another look.
```

```
Robert wrote:
> On Thu, 11 Dec 2008 10:04:02 -0600, "Paul H" <NoSpamphobergNoSpam@att.net> wrote:
> 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> No, C is like Cobol, not assembly language.

At any rate, C is closer to COBOL than it is to most assembly
languages. (The "C is portable assembly language" cliche started as a
hyperbolic metaphor for C's combination of some portability and
low-level operations; by now it's just silly.)

> But Pete said C#, which is like Java.

Certainly closer to Java than to C. Though it's gotten pretty far from
its Java roots, particularly when you take frameworks into account.

Robert's correct that Java and C# are relatively high-level languages
with decent abstraction and expressiveness. Both are available free of
charge (other than your own costs for downloading and learning them,
of course), and there are various tutorials available online that
could give you a taste.
```

```
On Dec 12, 5:04 am, "Paul H" <NoSpamphobergNoS...@att.net> wrote:
> Thanks Pete,
> But your alternatives mostly intimidate me.

You should note that while OpenCobol, Cobol-IT, tinyCobol are mostly
ANS85 they may not have features that you think of a 'normal'.
MicroFocus has many extensions, such as X/Open and ADIS. These are not
standard ANS85 Cobol and may not exist in other compilers.

For example in Microfocus you can ACCEPT and DISPLAY AT position (or
variations such as LINE COLUMN) to produce screen interactive
programs.

In Microfocus (and other commercial products) you can have shared
files with record locking.

These are not standard ANS85 and may not be available in other
compilers, or may not work the same as MF or other commercial products
(RM, Acu, etc).

> Isn't using C or Java like
> coding in assembly language?  

No. Using GOTO or not using scope terminators in Cobol is "like using
assembly language".

> If I stay with COBOL, none of the alternatives
> offer the debugging environment like MF's.  Animating, breakpoints, value
> monitoring, etc.  These make me productive.

> What do you think about
> Clarion?  I did buy a copy a few years ago, but gave up trying to learn to
> use it.  Maybe I'll give it another look.

> Paul
>
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: I need a new compiler...

_(reply depth: 7)_

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2008-12-12T12:24:18-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ghufml31br2@news1.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<3525k4p68a68p3bk30vjkp29poiba2uvv9@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<494095a5$0$5502$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <6qcd9oFbr6ltU1@mid.individual.net> <494139f5$0$5477$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <d9d518da-09d2-48d5-8382-81f0f3fe9f05@i18g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <ghtgau$ket$1@reader1.panix.com> <3525k4p68a68p3bk30vjkp29poiba2uvv9@4ax.com>`

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> On Fri, 12 Dec 2008 11:01:50 +0000 (UTC), docdwarf@panix.com () wrote:
> 
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> not as much fun as Action! or Forth which I had for the same computer.

The distinction between assembled and compiled languages is vague at best.

The iSeries (nee AS/400) has an "MI" ("Machine Instruction") language
that looks like assembly. It's what iSeries compilers produce, or at
least what they used to produce back when I did a lot of AS/400 work.
But what it generates are not machine instructions for the POWER/AS
CPUs that those machines run. MI is a CISC language and POWER/AS is a
RISC chip with an instruction set that doesn't look anything like MI;
and because the iSeries is a capability architecture, you don't really
want people generating arbitrary object code anyway.

There's a .NET CLI "assembler" that lets you create programs from the
low-level CLI instructions. Those aren't the instruction set of the
actual physical CPU; it's a virtual assembly language.

Run an emulator such as Hercules and assemble and run a program under
it. Those instructions aren't "machine code" for the physical CPU
running them either.

The "esoteric" (aka "evil") language Brainfuck is a low-level language
where each "statement" corresponds to a single instruction of the
virtual machine. It's a very primitive and bizarrely arbitrary virtual
machine, but there's a one-to-one mapping.

The even more esoteric language Unlambda is rather low-level too, and
all of its builtins are primitive operations. Of course, they're
operations like the s-combinator and k-combinator, but the each have a
concise formal definition and do a single thing.

Which of those is assembly language?
```

```
Bill Gunshannon wrote:
> In article <34h5k4td4l98q98308mkg2ri53h0raakvg@4ax.com>,
> 	Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net> writes:
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> a couple of decades ago.  Yet another wheel re-invented.

In what way was this reinventing a wheel? The various proposed Java
"accelerator" chip designers were well aware of other implementations
of pseudocode engines in hardware, and similar projects. (There were
hardware LISP implementations, hardware APL implementations, hardware
simulators for other machines...)

None of those executed Java bytecode, so of course they had to create
new hardware to do that.

When I write a program, the existence of other programs doesn't mean
I'm reinventing the program wheel.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: I need a new compiler...

_(reply depth: 5)_

- **From:** btiffin@canada.com
- **Date:** 2008-12-13T12:08:22-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<30997c16-9a6c-48c3-825e-291ac7d9aa22@e22g2000vbe.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<494095a5$0$5502$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <49409903$0$5487$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <6qcd9oFbr6ltU1@mid.individual.net> <494139f5$0$5477$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <d9d518da-09d2-48d5-8382-81f0f3fe9f05@i18g2000prf.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Dec 11, 3:33 pm, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
> On Dec 12, 5:04 am, "Paul H" <NoSpamphobergNoS...@att.net> wrote:
>
…[33 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Recent OpenCOBOL 1.1 pre-release versions have a fair handle on SCREEN
SECTION and DISPLAY var LINE nn COLUMN nn WITH ERASE EOS etc...

As far as I know, the only parts of the NIST test suite not passed now
are REPORT SECTION and COMMUNICATION SECTION.

With Berkeley DB, most of the sophisticated record and key management
options are supported and there are efforts afoot for a native VBISAM
layer to make the BDB dependency optional.

We now have layers for embedding JavaScript core (SpiderMonkey), Lua,
Perl, Tcl/Tk, libCURL, Regina REXX, SQLite (both API and a shell
clone), libDBI for MySQL and other databases.  Bindings to a full
fledged GUI through GTK+ are in progress and even in its early
version, support for windows, buttons, text entry, file dialogs
etcetera allow for simple but useful GUI applications.  Due to
OpenCOBOL's model of C generation there are already linkage options
for gnat Ada, gfortran and the plethora of other languages that
generate object files.  Routines for access to gnuplot have been
written, along with shell scripts and other external tools.

A very complete ncurses layer has existed for quite sometime in the
cobcurses package.

Documentation is being written and grows daily including instructions
for using the gdb debugger.

Oh, and it's a very comprehensive COBOL compiler as is, and
development is so active at the moment that it is hard to keep up.  ;)

I recommend that anyone and everyone give it a go.   http://opencobol.org

A repository of pre-built binaries exist for AIX, HPUX, WIN32 and I've
been running it actively on Debian GNU/Linux since I first bumped into
it a few scant months ago.  Information about the repository can be
found in the opencobol.org forums.

Cheers,
Brian
```

```
On 2008-12-10 22:22:57 -0600, "Paul H" <NoSpamphobergNoSpam@att.net> said:

worth it. No runtime costs.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: I need a new compiler...

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-12-11T22:28:58-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

```
On Dec 12, 5:15 pm, Paul <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote:
> On 2008-12-10 22:22:57 -0600, "Paul H" <NoSpamphobergNoS...@att.net> said:
>
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> By the way, C is pretty much based upon PDP-11 Macro Assembler.

Where did that come from ? C was derived from B which was a
simplified version of BCPL. BCPL stands for 'Basic CPL' (Basic as in
fundemental and not BASIC). CPL was Combined Programming Language, a
combination of APL (Atlas, not the IBM APL) amd ACL (Atlas Commercial
Language) with much influence from Algol 60, produced at the Atlas
Laboratory.


> It
> isn't hard, you just have to master a few little details. Now using it
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> -Paul
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: I need a new compiler...

- **From:** PR <paul.raulerson@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2008-12-12T12:45:45-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1f8384b5-4b11-4cff-aee1-ac46898f687e@q30g2000prq.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<494095a5$0$5502$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <4941e558$0$5493$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <fe80d49a-1c23-4a56-a825-4ca4a6a2ba82@z6g2000pre.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Dec 12, 12:28 am, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
> On Dec 12, 5:15 pm, Paul <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote:
>
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
>

C was written at Bell Labs from 1969 to 1972, on an early PDP-7 and
later, around 1970, on a PDP-11. C was to be the systems programming
language for Unix. C does owe much to BCPL as a grandparent language.
The 'B' language in it's lineage is still a little open to debate -
instead of a contraction of BCP it might be a contraction of the
Bonnie or BON language. You can guess who that language is named after
I suppose. However, there is no doubt that B was influenced by BCPL.

However, C owes much more to it's PDP parentage. It was originally
designed to be a language that was close to the machine, and in fact
it is. There are plenty of 1 to 1 references to PDP Macro language in
it, the most obvious being the increment and decrement use of ++ and
--. Pointer arithmetic being another.

Just go write a few thousand lines of PDP-11 Macro Assembler and you
will never again doubt that C is in fact a "portable assembler" and
based upon PDP-11 Macro.

-Paul


> > It
> > isn't hard, you just have to master a few little details. Now using it
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> > -Paul
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: I need a new compiler...

_(reply depth: 4)_

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-12-12T14:58:53-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<e22cb50a-e4e2-4f8d-9b96-d10dc4448471@w39g2000prb.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<494095a5$0$5502$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <4941e558$0$5493$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <fe80d49a-1c23-4a56-a825-4ca4a6a2ba82@z6g2000pre.googlegroups.com> <1f8384b5-4b11-4cff-aee1-ac46898f687e@q30g2000prq.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Dec 13, 9:45 am, PR <paul.rauler...@gmail.com> wrote:
> On Dec 12, 12:28 am, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
>
…[31 more quoted lines elided]…
> I suppose. However, there is no doubt that B was influenced by BCPL.

> However, C owes much more to it's PDP parentage. It was originally
> designed to be a language that was close to the machine, and in fact
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>

From "The Bell System Technical Journal" July-August 1978 in an
article "The C Programming Language" by Kernighan, Ritchie an Johnson:

"C was originally written for the PDP-11 under Unix ...", not PDP-7
though the original Unix was written on and for that (or was it a
PDP-9 as some source have it).

"... but the language is not tied to any particular hardware or
operating system."

"C was developed for the PDP-11 on the Unix system in 1972."

You may note that many Unix applications were written in C before Unix
itself was rewritten, In fact the 'Unix Portability Project' in which
substancial portions of Unix were written in C targetted the Interdata
8/32'.


"The C language of today is the product of several years of evolution.
Many of its most important ideas stem from the considerably older, but
still quite vital, language BCPL developed by Martin Richards. The
influence of BCPL on C proceeded indirectly through the language B,
which was written by Ken Thompson in 1970 for the first Unix system on
the PDP-11."

"Although neither B nor C could really be considered dialects of BCPL,
both share several characteristic features with it:

...."

"B and BCPL differ mainly in their syntax, ..."

[much discussion of B, BCPL and C including examples follows]

What needs to be established is, for example, when PDP Macro Assembler
gained the use of '++' and ---' operators. One may well have copied
from the other, or it may have been common usage in other languages of
the time as well.



> -Paul
>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<49432d4e$0$5484$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net>`
- **References:** `<494095a5$0$5502$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <4941e558$0$5493$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <fe80d49a-1c23-4a56-a825-4ca4a6a2ba82@z6g2000pre.googlegroups.com> <1f8384b5-4b11-4cff-aee1-ac46898f687e@q30g2000prq.googlegroups.com> <e22cb50a-e4e2-4f8d-9b96-d10dc4448471@w39g2000prb.googlegroups.com>`

```
On 2008-12-12 16:58:53 -0600, Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> said:

> On Dec 13, 9:45ï¿½am, PR <paul.rauler...@gmail.com> wrote:
>> On Dec 12, 12:28ï¿½am, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
…[87 more quoted lines elided]…
> the time as well.

Not really- PDP-11s *always* had the ++ and -- operators; the PDP-7 did 
not. It did, however, have some memory locations that would 
autoincrement with an indirect reference, and this is how the operators 
got into B- or rather into NB which was the direct ancestor of C.

You are correct (and in fact, I have the same reference journal 
here...) that C didn't become popular until it landed on and was 
developed more for the PDP-11. However, B and NB were actually usually 
compiled on a GECOS machine - I want to say GE-635, but that is from 
memory and I may be wrong.  There was a lot of influence from there as 
well.

You might want to look up a later reference as well; I suggest _History 
of Programming Languages-II; my copy was published by Addison-Wesly 
back in 1996. The ISBN is: 0-201-89502-1.

Dennis Ritchie has a paper published in there that clears a lot of this up. :)

Also the definitive book on BCPL is _BCPL: The Language and its 
Compiler by Marty Richards. No ISBN, but published by Cambridge 
University Press in 1979.  Co-Author was a chap named Strevens.  I 
picked up my copy for $1 at a used book store. <grin>

There is supposed to be an earlier manual or paper on it by Richards, 
from sometimes in the 1960's, but I have never seen it. I would love to 
find an electonic copy of that.

And just to tie the thread back to COBOL a tiny bit- a lot of Mainframe 
Assembler people learned Assembler by studying what the COBOL compilers 
generated.  It ain't NOTHING like PDP assembler...  or C! :)


-Paul


> 
> 
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>>>> -Paul
```

```
I suppose the categories "assembler" and "compiler" in general have
lost their usefulness.   They were largely marketing anyway. Nowadays
we have tools that don't fit the categories very well.

When IDMS came out with multiple parents, to separate itself from IMS,
Cullinet/Cullinane called it a "Network Database", instead of a
"Hierarchal database".   But at this late date, Hierarchal fits
better.   That said, many marketing terms have become part of our
language - for instance the made-up word "cemetery" has pretty much
replaced "graveyard".  (Wide shoulder coffins have pretty much died
out, replaced by caskets - still everybody recognizes the traditional
coffin shape, even if such coffins aren't made anymore (that I know
of)).

I liked it when IBM "invented" virtual memory.
```

```
Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message
news:ln4dk45di2426735qdfmts6qn42stlgh3o@4ax.com...
> I liked it when IBM "invented" virtual memory.
>


I was working for Univac at the time, and we had a good deal of trouble
convincing prospects that re-entrant ("stateless" for the newer folk) was
better than virtual memory - and in the case of multiple simultanous users
of the same program, such as on-line apps, it did save a lot of I/O
thrashing - but Univac aquired RCA, which had vm and had been marketing it
for some time before IBM took notice, so we were able to go to our prospects
and smile at them and say "You know all those bad things we were saying
about VM last week?  They don't matter any more!  Because we have VM too!"
Didn't do us any good that I can recall because IBM customers were IBM
customers, come hell or high water.

IBM was to a certain extent unclear on the concept then; the lowest model of
the 370 line would run 128m (or was it 256m?) in 128k of physical memory!
Had to chain the disk drives to the floor!

PL
```

```
Leaving aside the 'all ya gotta do is...' I find in the second sentence...

bias of my instructors; others may argue strongly for the advantages to be 
found in writing your batch quarterly inventory reports (early 1970s,
```

- **References:** `<494095a5$0$5502$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <4941e558$0$5493$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <ghtno4$hvh$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
> 
> the first resonates; as I recall it Ritchie designed C at Bell Labs (back 
> when it was Bell Labs, early 1970s) as a replacement for Assembley 
> mnemonics.

Compiled languages in general were designed as a replacement for
assembly programming. That doesn't make them "like assembly".

>  It is a compiled language, true, but still provides low-level
> system/memory/device access in an Assembley-like manner.

No, it doesn't. Some C implementations may extend the language to
provide direct access to memory and devices (whatever that may mean on
the platform), but that's not part of the C language.
```

```
Actually, yes it it. Access to memory is always defined in C, as
pointers are intrinsic. And pointers are not special variable types,
they originally were integers, and for the most part, still are.

This more than anything else, separates C from the Algol language
family, especially in any of its modern implementations.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: I need a new compiler...

_(reply depth: 5)_

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2008-12-15T11:32:41-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gi63hl224ng@news7.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<c09e0f88-3c0c-4fb8-bfc4-b876eb7a748c@z28g2000prd.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<494095a5$0$5502$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <4941e558$0$5493$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <ghtno4$hvh$1@reader1.panix.com> <ghufmm41br2@news1.newsguy.com> <c09e0f88-3c0c-4fb8-bfc4-b876eb7a748c@z28g2000prd.googlegroups.com>`

```
PR wrote:
> On Dec 12, 11:29 am, Michael Wojcik <mwoj...@newsguy.com> wrote:
>> docdw...@panix.com wrote:
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> Actually, yes it it.

No, it is not. I suggest you read ISO 9899-1999 (plus Technical
Corregida), which is the definition of the C language. You will find
that it does not require implementations to "provide low-level
system/memory/device access", and indeed does not define any language
constructs which remotely resemble anything such "access" might include.

> Access to memory is always defined in C, as
> pointers are intrinsic.

C pointers do not need to provide access, "low-level" or otherwise, to
physical memory or devices. They need to support the dereference
operator, and so could be considered as providing access to "memory"
in the sense of the abstract machine, but there is *absolutely no
requirement* that such access have any particular relationship to the
hardware.

> And pointers are not special variable types,

Indeed. They have pointer type, which is not "special" (whatever that
might mean). But it's not any other kind of type, either.

> they originally were integers, and for the most part, still are.

Originally, yes. As the language is currently defined, no. Type
punning between pointers and integers invokes Undefined Behavior. That
it works in some implementations is beside the point.
```

```
Paul wrote:
> On 2008-12-15 10:32:41 -0600, Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com> said:
>> PR wrote:
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> ratified with C89, and are maintained through all current implementations.

And no part of the standard requires that pointers or the address or
indirection operators provide access to system memory. Feel free to
cite c&v if you believe otherwise.

>>> Access to memory is always defined in C, as
>>> pointers are intrinsic.
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> operator.

Pointers are not "address operators" in any way, shape, or form. There
is one address operator (&), and there are various cases where other
constructs decay to addresses.

But, once more, those addresses are purely defined in terms of the
virtual machine and do not have any required correspondence with the
physical machine.

ISO 9899-1999 5.1.2.3 #1: "The semantic descriptions in this
International Standard describe the behavior of an
abstract machine in which issues of optimization are irrelevant."

> There is very much an absolute requirement that they do.

Care to show us where in ISO 9899 that requirement appears?

> You are arguing C++, which is a totally different animal indeed.

I most certainly am not. ISO 9899 is the C specification.

>>> And pointers are not special variable types,
>>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> Pointers are address operators, always.

Pointers are not operators in C, so your claim is invalid by definition.

>  If you don't know what
> "special" means in terms of language and compile construction, then look
> it up. The usage of the term is both precise and accurate.

Complete rot. "Special type" has no single special meaning as a term
of art in computation theory or compiler design. The references I have
to hand use it in a variety of ways, or not at all.

>> Originally, yes. As the language is currently defined, no. Type
>> punning between pointers and integers invokes Undefined Behavior. That
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> cast.

You really can't be bothered to check, can you?

6.3.2.3 #5: "An integer may be converted to any pointer type. Except
as previously specified, the result is implementation-defined, might
not be correctly aligned, might not point to an entity of the
referenced type, and might be a trap representation."

And since this conversion is not a constraint violation, conforming
implementations are not required to issue a diagnostic, so you're
wrong on that count, too.

> You can certainly cast an address to an integer.

I never claimed otherwise.
```

```
On Dec 17, 4:27 pm, Paul <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote:

You have broadened the argument to 'access memory'. Name one useful
language that does not allow an 'access to memory'.

Here's an example of an 'access to memory':

          MOVE Var1   TO Var2

What was _actually_ being discussed was 'low-level' access, or access
to _system_ memory.

In many cases a C program cannot access arbitrary memory areas and has
no facility, not even pointers, to access outside the memory area that
it has been assigned by the operating system, just like many other
languages.


> >> You are arguing C++, which is a totally different animal indeed.
>
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…

They are not "operators", &, +, =, are. Pointers are not.

In many cases pointers may be addresses. Whether they are physical
addresses or some other, such as a virtual address, depends on the
system and the implementation.


> but arguing in circles won't make
> any difference.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

are not those of the PDP-11.

Even if on a PDP-11 an arbitrary address in a pointer can be used to
access system memory that does not mean that every implementation of C
can access system memory by manipulation of pointers.


> And are manipulated the same was a PDP-11 GPR. As such, they are also
> operators tht
> serve to provide indirect memory references.
```

```
Richard wrote:
> On Dec 17, 4:27 pm, Paul <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote:
>> On 2008-12-16 14:35:39 -0600, Michael Wojcik <mwoj...@newsguy.com>
…[202 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Well done, Richard! If there was such a slant I believe you would be among 
the first to see it : - )

There most certainly has been no such slant in Michael's responses.

Paul is obviously a devotee of Programmable Digital Processors (a name 
invented to avoid calling them "computers", and so permit their export to 
countries the US had blacklisted for computer export during the Cold War), 
and while these were/are fine machines, that really isn't what the argument 
is about.

Michael can see abstraction and recognise it, Paul apparently has difficulty 
in this area. Both men are obviously capable, and it is a pity to see the 
discussion descending into flaming.

Personally, I can accept Michael's argument regarding C being based on the 
concept of a virtual machine (although I haven't come across that view 
before) and pointers therefore not necessarily being tied to system memory. 
(I liked the idea of the fairy going off into the forest and retrieving the 
data from the magic mushroom... so much of what we do with computers is like 
that :-)) Layers of abstraction help to keep us sane. If anyone actually 
sits down and tries to visualise what REALLY happens when a program is 
executed (as I once did in the 60s after dropping LSD... the first and last 
time I ever did that), it is simply incomprehensible and terrifying... (acid 
or no acid...) The speeds involved alone, are outside of the model of time 
which most of us can actually conceptualize.

(Although you can intellectually visualize nano and pico seconds, you cannot 
experience them...People who have worked with cameras will realize that 
1000th of a second is about the limit of experience, and that is being 
reinforced by the sound the camera makes when it fires, which is actually 
longer than 1000th of a second. For most of us, we can only truly relate to 
events that are longer than around a 20th of a second (50 milliseconds - an 
Ice Age for a CPU))

I find all the time  in posts which I make here, which are abstract or 
conceptual, many people simply don't get it. It isn't because they are 
stupid (far from it...) it is only because they have different models of how 
things work You give some sample code and (provided it is not too esoteric 
or unfamiliar) immediately the pennies start to drop.

Paul, on the other hand has spent many years working with a particular 
programming environment and, like all of us, has created certain mental 
models that make it possible to deal with it. For him pointers relate to 
something tangible and concrete that he can know and understand even outside 
of the C language. He only has to open the covers on his PDP to see memory 
sitting there, solid and real. He can write assembler language and know that 
it relates to the architecture he knows and loves. It is easy to project C 
into this category also.

Who is right?

In a broader view, without being pedantic, I think they both are (although I 
prefer Michael's view, because I like abstraction).

The fact is that "rightness" may be a very subjective thing. Paul's view 
does not stop him being a very capable programmer any more than Michael's 
prevents him from being one either.

The way an individual perceives somethng is only "wrong" if it prevents a 
desired result from being achieved. In this case neither of the "models" 
cause bad or incorrect program code to be produced.

It is apparently very hard for people who program computers to NOT commit to 
THEIR mental model as being the only one because they have used it for 
decades and it works. We are also conditioned by life in general to the idea 
that if something is right and works, then any deviation from the approach 
that achieved it must be wrong.

Does it matter whether a C pointer references a memory location or a magic 
mushroom, as long as the correct data is retrieved? Obviously, it doesn't.

But that is only true as far as data retrieval goes.  It may be that the 
"mushroom model" helps when discussing architectures which are not yet 
realized, and won't be unless they are discussed... This is the point about 
conceptualization through abstraction.


>
>> I wonder if you are the same guy that was arguing that .NET was the
>> first really object oriented
>> design platform a year or two ago?

No, that was probably me... : - )

Pete.
```

```
On 2008-12-17 16:18:38 -0600, "Pete Dashwood" 
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> said:

> Richard wrote:
>> On Dec 17, 4:27 pm, Paul <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote:
…[218 more quoted lines elided]…
> discussion descending into flaming.

Well, I can see abstraction. I just usually an an advocate of object 
oriented *design* over object oriented programming. I think things need 
to be implemented with an eye to efficiency as well as maintainability 
and such.

But I would not argue that abstraction, encapsulation, de-coupling, and 
a few other methodologies are at the core of good design and even good 
programming.

But at the end of the day, they are all boiled down to zeros and ones, 
opcodes, operands, and all the rest of the interesting little hardware 
details.

PDP-11s are favorites of mine because I worked on hardware and software 
for Singler Link flight simulators.

> 
> Personally, I can accept Michael's argument regarding C being based on the
> concept of a virtual machine (although I haven't come across that view
> before) and pointers therefore not necessarily being tied to system memory.

Oh, C does present a rudimentary virtual machine  - that's tru of just 
about any high level language. Perhaps of most assembly languages too.  
But the machine it happens to present is pretty much a PDP-11.  PDP-11 
references *are* to memory. Like I said, show me a C implementation 
that doesn't tie pointers to memory and I'll give it some serious 
thought.

I was not feeling well yesterday and it was unwarranted of me to get a 
bit testy I suppose.

> 
> (I liked the idea of the fairy going off into the forest and retrieving the
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> which most of us can actually conceptualize.

Not sure I agree with that Pete. Then again, I work with mainframe 
assembler every day, and am the system programmer for the mainframe, 
Linux admin, and general cook and bottlewasher besides.

My guilty secret is that I really like RealBasic- a *lot*. It is very 
object oriented, but not so complex as Visual Basic, and it compiles 
and runs on Macs and Linux as well as Windows.

Then again, I really love COBOL, C, Ada, Pascal, Assembler, RPG, and 
several assembler languages, as well as Smalltalk, Modula-2, Oberon, 
Haskell, and PERL, Java, and JavaScript.

I do not like Python or PHP, which my web guys swear by. :)
 I'm learning to like Fortran.

Makes me an odd duck I guess.

> 
> (Although you can intellectually visualize nano and pico seconds, you cannot
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> Ice Age for a CPU))

Ah. I see those as mathematical concepts, which are as real to me as 
anything I can directly perceive. In other words, I can compute a 
duration. I had enormous trouble with math as a kid until I caught onto 
that trick.

> 
> I find all the time  in posts which I make here, which are abstract or
…[39 more quoted lines elided]…
> conceptualization through abstraction.

MMM- we disagree here as well Pete. Abstract models do give one a grasp 
on an architecture, but running hard simulations of that architecture 
turn abstract ideas into a more concrete reality, which is useful in 
either implementing or designing the implementation of that archtecture.

Modern computers are so darn complex, they have to simulated before 
they re designed if for no other reason than to control cost and 
attempt to avoid very costly mistakes.

In other words, I think the SmallTalk guys missed a bet with Object 
Oriented programming - the design is certainly best dealt with via 
abstraction. But using some methodology like layered virtual machines 
to implement allows one to use more and more concrete tools the lower 
down into the implementation you go.

Of course, that is also a bit of an 80's and 90's viewpoint as well.

Newer methods, like RAD and "Extreme Programming" *also* work. And when 
they work, they work very well indeed. They only begin to fail when the 
complexity of a project or project segment exceeds the abilty of a 
single programmer to envision the entire segement.

Traditional programming also has the same issue; and is why daa 
abstraction, decoupling, and other methodologies were essentially 
standardized. They are not much more than the codified best practices 
of programmers who learned how to get through incredibly complex tasks.



> 
> 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> No, that was probably me... : - )

<grin>

> 
> Pete.
```

```
I imagine that would be fascinating work. Don't know if you are aware, but 
there is a big community of "Simmers" who build their own flight simulators, 
particularly in the USA. I have a friend in California who has the nose 
section of a Boeing 737 in his garage and has been gradually building the 
functionality back into it. He is running MS Flight simulator 2000+ on 
several attached servers and they are driving the actual gauges and dials on 
the cockpit fascia.  The controls are all plugged into the program with 
hydraulic dampers giving tactile feedback, and he is currently getting 
scenery projection onto the cockpit windows.  It is really excellent to play 
with.  He insisted I have a go (I have a Private Pilots Licence) and I 
crashed the first time I attempted to land a Cessna at LAX. After that I 
managed to make a decidedly shaky landing in Hawaii, and then a slightly 
better one on McCarran field in Las Vegas (which I have actually done for 
real). He has become an accomplished pilot on the simulator and I was very 
impressed at his knowledge, understanding, and skill. Yet he is terrified of 
flying and won't travekl by air. Over the last three years this hobby has 
probably cost him around $US12,000, (the nose shell was much cheaper than 
you might think, bought from one of the desert graveyards; major cost was 
getting it delivered to the huse...) But I should think he could sell the 
simulator for many times that. He told me there is a community of 
programmers (he is not one) who provide the Sim community with customised 
plugins to the FS program.
>

I respect and understand your disagreement, but just think about it for a 
moment... If you don't use an analog but (just like drilling through the 
virtual machine abstraction) go down to the innate actual movement of 
electricity (whatever the Hell THAT is...electron flow, photons, energy 
carrying wavicles - all devices Physicists use to REPRESENT underlying 
reality) then you get into paradoxes like things happening many times faster 
than the speed you can observe or think about them, never mind correlating 
or considering the results...  Quantum uncertainty is "bad" enough, but this 
is even more mind-blowing... don't stay there too long : - )
```

```
On 2008-12-17 22:12:35 -0600, "Pete Dashwood" 
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> said:

…[38 more quoted lines elided]…

!!!  No, I was not aware of this, but I assure you, I soon will be.

>> 
>>> 
…[46 more quoted lines elided]…
> We need abstraction layers to keep us sane.

Oh certainly so - but just like in math, at any level of abstraction, 
you have to
assume some things are axioms - or in other words, concrete. It is 
merely a shift
of viewpoint to change which of those items is concrete.

Your electrical example is a good one; the physics of power propagation is
confusing - it can even seem that the propagation of electric potential appears
to propagate instantaneously from the generator source to the point 
where it is to
be applied. Confusing indeed!

But in software, when you switch layers of abstractions, different "objects"
become more or less concrete.

At top level design, a display can be a very abstract thing. We talk in 
terms of calculations,
invoices, displays, etc. All of which are "concrete" in that they are 
real things, but are very
abstract in terms of the software.

As implementation proceeds, not only does additional detail fall out, 
but those the top level
design objects become more and more concrete. Perhaps an invoice is 
initially represented
by an invoice object, data structure, class, or module.

As you drill down into the detail of that "invoice", it becomes ever 
more concrete. Practically,
you are writing code or defining data that can actually execute on the 
computer.

You can drill down all the way to 1s and 0s if you need to, which in 
computer terms, is about
as concrete as it gets.

Layered Virtual Machines / Object Oriented Development is the best 
methodology I know of
to do that, and it is where I tend to draw my preference for object 
oriented design that drives the
implementation in ever more detailed (and concrete!) layers.

It's kinda old by today's standards, but every time I have ever applied 
it, or seen it applied to a
project, the project has been very successful.

> 
> 
…[34 more quoted lines elided]…
> abstraction.

Exactly so- the trick is to adjust your level of abstraction. To deal 
with math,
you often have to assume that some abstraction is concrete - like an axiom.

>> 
>>> 
…[50 more quoted lines elided]…
> manipulating mathematical symbols is useless for problem solving.

Oh certes!  Simulation is an abstraction. It is just a much more concrete
abstraction than a model. In other words, it implements an abstract model.

>> 
>> Modern computers are so darn complex, they have to simulated before
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> build them.

I almost always do that myself. :)

> 
>> 
…[28 more quoted lines elided]…
> waiting to catch you when you get too smug... : - )

I rather expect your capacity for dealing effectively with abstract ideas and
models is somewhat greater than the average joe. :)


>>

…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> Pete.
```

- **References:** `<494095a5$0$5502$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <4941e558$0$5493$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <ghtno4$hvh$1@reader1.panix.com> <ghufmm41br2@news1.newsguy.com> <c09e0f88-3c0c-4fb8-bfc4-b876eb7a748c@z28g2000prd.googlegroups.com> <gi63hl224ng@news7.newsguy.com> <49471cf4$0$5498$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <gi94kb0qt9@news2.newsguy.com> <494871be$0$5502$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <7cae1f08-e635-4b0a-88b0-789c424be7a9@a26g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <6qtc5uFeqqnqU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Right. Let's not let this get any more heated.

I am talking about C as it's defined by the specification. My
inclination is to be a language lawyer (as Fred Brooks would have it);
so as far as I'm concerned, the C language is the ideal language
defined by ISO 9899-1999 (plus corrections), and implementations only
approach it. (I'll note that this view is shared by most of the
regulars in comp.lang.c, comp.lang.c.moderated, and comp.std.c.)

I say this as someone who has been a professional programmer, mostly
in C, for more than two decades. I started with K&R C (since there was
no ANSI or ISO standard twenty years ago), and I've worked with a wide
range of K&R, C89/C90, C94, and C99 implementations, including many
with various extensions and deficiencies. I've written C for platforms
ranging from small embedded systems to mainframes and large parallel
machines.

I learned C from K&R1; I own, and am very familiar with, K&R2 and the
ANSI publications of the C90 and C99 standards, plus their related
materials (such as the Rationale). I have a pretty extensive library
of C materials, such as van der Linden's _Expert C Programming_,
Plauger's _The Standard C Library_ and Jones' monumental _The New C
Standard: An Economic and Cultural Commentary_, which at over 1600
pages is probably the most thoroughgoing analysis of the C standard
we'll ever see. (There are a couple of pages just on the requirement
that integer types be represented in a pure binary encoding.)

In my work I've found a strict from-the-standard interpretation of the
language useful. It makes for portable code that takes into account
the latitude allowed to conforming implementations. It provides
leverage for requesting fixes from vendors when implementations don't
conform. It supplies a standard nomenclature and authority when
discussing obscure cases with other experts.

Other programmers, such as Paul, may of course view the language
differently. C is not solely defined by the standard; obviously any
practitioner can define it in whatever way appeals, and there are many
cases (eg when targeting only a particular platform with special
extensions) when it makes sense to deviate from the standard language.

So, to return to the original idea that started this thread, there are
programmers writing C programs that do not treat the language as
providing "low-level access to memory and devices"; and there are C
implementations that do not provide such a feature (at least for
reasonable definitions of "low-level access"). But it is also true
that C has traditionally often - perhaps mostly - been used as a
low-level language, and some may prefer to define the language by
those sorts of implementations and applications, regardless of what
the standard says.
```

```
'what is the difference between arrays and pointers' 
http://c-faq.com/aryptr/practdiff.html .  From that page:

--begin quoted text:

A pointer is a reference to any data element (of a particular type) 
anywhere.

--end quoted text

Now... if the reference provided by a pointer is one towards low-level 
memory (a sub-set of 'anywhere') and if having a reference to a data 
element allows for access to the data then C would appear, via 
pointers, to allow low-level access to memory... but this, of course, is 
Logic, not necessarily Computer Programming.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: I need a new compiler...

_(reply depth: 9)_

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2008-12-16T15:54:48-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gi94kc2qt9@news2.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<gi6slm$1ml$1@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<494095a5$0$5502$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <gi63hm324ng@news7.newsguy.com> <gi6ijn$a0h$1@reader1.panix.com> <e0kdk4pq6b46660uq2b7shvoc7lrs3ljm4@4ax.com> <gi6slm$1ml$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
> 
> It may well be that the ISO or IEEE or ANSI or whatever standard does not 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> quacking like a duck comes to mind.

Since this empirical test is trivially shown to fail, it's a moot point.

Many C implementations execute in a process-virtual environment, so at
best they can only provide access to process virtual memory. Maybe
that's "low-level" to some, but there are a great many turtles below it.

There are C implementations where pointers are not machine addresses
at all, notably the various implementations for the AS/400. On those
machines, C pointers were 128-bit tokens that identified objects in
the single-level store, but they did not contain machine addresses,
which were 64- or 65-bit (for the CISC- and RISC-architecture machines
respectively).

When C99 was first approved, Peter Seebach claimed to be the only
conforming implementation. He spoke in jest, of course, but he had a
copy of the standard and fit the definition. And while it's hard to
prove this definitively, I suspect most phenomenologists would tell
you that few of us have low-level access to our own memories.
```

```
Michael Wojick,
  I can't tell.  Have you been debating a "real-world" issue or a philosophical 
one?

In other words, are there existing implementations of C that (as required) 
support "pointers" but do NOT  allow (or support) pointers that "get the 
program" to system-level storage?

I am NOT a C programmer, but when I have interacted with those that do use it, 
it seems to me that all implementations that they use DO allow the C program to 
access (at least "read") data at "system controlled" (low-level?) storage.

It is also true, that some "systems" do NOT allow the "average" C (or Assembler 
or whatever) program to MODIFY "system-controlled" storage, but that is a 
different question (to me) than accessing such storage.

If there are C implementations that do not allow programmers to "read" the 
storage where "system control blocks, etc" reside, I would be interested in 
which C and on what platforms these exist.

If you are just stating that the C Standard does not REQUIRE this, then that may 
be "nice to know" - but is different from what actual implementations DO.
```

```
On Dec 17, 12:02 pm, "William M. Klein" <wmkl...@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
wrote:
> <much debate snipped>
>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> program" to system-level storage?

Many, the world is no longer running on Z80s, or even 8086s.

In those any program could create an address that accessed 'low level'
memory and could poke around the operating system.

On the 80286 (as well as many others) a pointer was not a physical
address but was a 'selector' plus an offset. The program was only
allowed, by the hardware, to use particular selectors which made up
their process image. Attempts to create addresses by making up an
arbitrary pointer value resulted in exceptions (abend).


> I am NOT a C programmer, but when I have interacted with those that do use it,
> it seems to me that all implementations that they use DO allow the C program to
> access (at least "read") data at "system controlled" (low-level?) storage.
>

Any program has the potential to read system controlled storage to the
extent that the operating system provides this. There may be system
calls which return a handle to particular data items.


> It is also true, that some "systems" do NOT allow the "average" C (or Assembler
> or whatever) program to MODIFY "system-controlled" storage, but that is a
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>

If the only way to access the 'system control blocks' is by making a
system call that returns a handle to this then potentially _any_
language can do that.

There are systems that (such as CP/M on a Z80 in the 70s or MS-DOS in
the 80s) where it is trivial to access the operating system memory
using fixed addresses, but the majority of systems (including Windows)
run processes where accessing arbitrary addresses only results in
accessing the process' own memory or an abend.


> If you are just stating that the C Standard does not REQUIRE this, then that may
> be "nice to know" - but is different from what actual implementations DO.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>  wmklein <at> ix.netcom.com
```

```
LA	R2,VAR1
ST 	R2,VAR2
LA	R2 VAR2

R2 is pretty much a handle, once the second LA has executed.
```

```
On Dec 17, 4:47 pm, Paul <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote:
> On 2008-12-16 19:35:32 -0600, "William M. Klein"
> <wmkl...@nospam.ix.netcom.com> said:
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
> R2 is pretty much a handle, once the second LA has executed.

'Handles' are usually _much_ more than that and cannot be used as you
claim. They are identifiers and _not_ machine addresses. They may be
implemented as an offset in a system table but it would be a mistake
to think that gives access to the table.

For example C file handles may be 0 (stdin), 1 (stdout), 2 (stdout)
and you _cannot_ use this as a pointer to get anything useful. The
handle can only be used as an identifier. Certainly it is likely that
this is used by the library routines as an index into the file handle
table to collect data which is then used to obtain the appropriate
information about the file, but it is completely wrong to assume that
a handle is a 'pointer' or that even if it is that what it points to
is a pointer.



> > As I tried to make clear in my earlier note, I fully understand that many (most
> > modern?) operating systems control access to their "internals" but I still
> > thought that in most of these systems you could get there (some how) in C.
>
>
```

```
On 2008-12-16 22:17:59 -0600, Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> said:

> On Dec 17, 4:47ï¿½pm, Paul <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote:
>> On 2008-12-16 19:35:32 -0600, "William M. Klein"
…[31 more quoted lines elided]…
> and you _cannot_ use this as a pointer to get anything useful.

Actually, those are dependent upon the operating system, but usually 
they are not file handles at all- they are file descriptors.  A file 
descriptor is by definition, an integer, and not a pointer.

However, if you do a fopen(), or you reopen a file descriptor, you will 
get back a file handle.

A file *handle* is a pointer to the address of a structure (record for 
COBOL folks :)  usually in the application memory space, that 
identifies a file, and keeps things like the file position and so forth 
available to the application. Just like a DCB.

That can vary by operating system, but a POSIX compliant OS will do 
things that way.

Microsoft is *not* POSIX compliant here.

Microsoft uses the term "file handle" to refer to a low level construct 
more like a POSIX file descriptor, and then provides some wrapper 
functions to support Microsoft file handles like integers.



> The
> handle can only be used as an identifier. Certainly it is likely that
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> is a pointer.

Again, Microsoft misused the term file handle, and had to supply 
functions to enable programs to use what Microsoft called a file handle 
as an integer.

In POSIX compliant operating systems, a file handle is, most 
definately, a pointer.

I understand that Micosoft is working to come into POSIX compliance in 
Windows 7. It was supposed to be in Vista, and is to some extent I 
think.


> 
> 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>  C.
```

```
Paul wrote:
> On 2008-12-16 22:17:59 -0600, Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> said:
>> On Dec 17, 4:47 pm, Paul <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote:
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> get back a file handle.

I think the problem here is that "handle" is not a word used by the C
standard, nor by the Single Unix Specification. Microsoft does use it
as a technical term, but other people have defined it differently.

The C standard and SUS use the terms "FILE object" and "stream" for
the object pointed to by a FILE*.

Of course some people do call a FILE* a "handle", but I've also seen
"handle" used for Unix/POSIX file descriptors. Mostly the term seems
to be used informally in the Unix world.

> That can vary by operating system, but a POSIX compliant OS will do
> things that way.
> 
> Microsoft is *not* POSIX compliant here.

To be fair, the Microsoft C runtimes have long (always?) included
fopen, etc. Since C90, of course, they're part of the C library and
have to be included by any conforming hosted implementation.

And in the Microsoft C implementation, a FILE* is a pointer to a
struct _iobuf, which is quite like the typical POSIX _iobuf. It refers
to the file using a small integer descriptor. This used to be an index
into the _iob array; these days that array doesn't have an identifier
in userspace, but instead is an anonymous array retrieved via a
function. But the descriptor is still an index, and all of this is in
process memory, again just like in POSIX.

Again, this is Microsoft's C library, which is different (though
implemented on top of) the Windows API.

> Microsoft uses the term "file handle" to refer to a low level construct
> more like a POSIX file descriptor, and then provides some wrapper
> functions to support Microsoft file handles like integers.

That's the Windows API. Windows handles are pointers, and there are
ways to create a C stream from a Windows file handle, or to extract
the file handle from a C stream, so the two APIs can interoperate.

> I understand that Micosoft is working to come into POSIX compliance in
> Windows 7. It was supposed to be in Vista, and is to some extent I think.

Windows has been POSIX-compliant since NT. POSIX support is
implemented through the POSIX subsystem, and hasn't been popular, but
it's there.
```

```
On 2008-12-16 17:02:31 -0600, "William M. Klein" 
<wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com> said:

> <much debate snipped>
> 
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
> be "nice to know" - but is different from what actual implementations DO.

If I understand what you are saying Bill, you are asking if C the 
language can access "system memory."

Technically, C can access any address the machine can provide access 
to, including any system storage.

Practially speaking, the Operating System, and sometimes the hardware, 
protects some areas of memory. This is especially true in all our 
modern systems like z/OS, Linux, z/VM, and even Windows, since 
applications run in a designed application space.

For example, on my zLinux systems, every application in the system 
loads and run from  address X'400000'. Of couse, that is a *virtual* 
address, which means there may be a thousand applcations running from 
the virtual address, but from 1000 different real addresses. It gets 
complex, because sometimes text (code) sections are designated 
read-only in memory, and only one copy of them is loaded. (This is 
Linux,
drives z/OS programmers crazy because they cannot store variable in the 
code section - at least if they ever intend to change those variable's 
values... :)

And then you get into shared memory segments. Take the same chunk of 
real memory, attach it to one or more applications - at different 
virtual addresses.

Like I said, it gets complex. Fun though. :)

Another point to consider, most of Unix, including the kernel level 
code (which by the way, usually *does* have access to every real memory 
address, system and application....)  is written in plain old C. Just a 
couple or three thousand lines of assembler, and the rest, millions of 
lines of code, in C.

Just out of curiousity - are there any OS's written in Cobol?
I don't know of any, but I bet there are one or two out there. :)
```

```
On 2008-12-16 22:25:13 -0600, Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> said:

> On Dec 17, 4:41ï¿½pm, Paul <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote:
>> On 2008-12-16 17:02:31 -0600, "William M. Klein"
…[76 more quoted lines elided]…
> assembler) that "*does* have access to every real memory address",

Because I look at the assembler code that the C compiler generates of course.
The following code will generate an error, because memory address 100 
is protected
by MacOS and UNIX.

#include <stdio.h>
int main(int argc, char *argv[]) {

  char *aCharPointer;
  char aCharVariable;

  aCharPointer = 100;
  aCharVariable = *aCharPointer;

  }

In Intel (or Wintel Parlance)

        .text
.globl _main
_main:
        pushl   %ebp
        movl    %esp, %ebp
        subl    $24, %esp
        movl    $100, -16(%ebp)
        movl    -16(%ebp), %eax
        movzbl  (%eax), %eax
        movb    %al, -9(%ebp)
        leave
        ret
        .subsections_via_symbols

Easier to see in mainframe GAS assembler I suppose:

        .file   "test.c"
.text
        .align  4
.globl main
        .type   main, @function
main:
.LFB3:
        stm     %r11,%r15,44(%r15)
.LCFI0:
        ahi     %r15,-112
.LCFI1:
        lr      %r11,%r15
.LCFI2:
        st      %r2,96(%r11)
        st      %r3,100(%r11)
        lhi     %r1,100
        st      %r1,104(%r11)
        l       %r1,104(%r11)
        mvc     108(1,%r11),0(%r1)
        lhi     %r1,0
        lr      %r2,%r1
        lm      %r11,%r15,156(%r11)
        br      %r14

The line I bolded above is the one that is doing "aCharVariable = 
*aCharPointer;"
A moments study will show that R1 has the value 100 in it, so in HLASM, 
it would
look like this:

LHI   R1,100
LA    R2,aCharVariable
MVI  0(R2),0(R1)

It is the same on Linux, VMS, AIX, MacOS, and so on, with varying 
degrees of complexity.

By the way, MVI 0(R2),0(R1) is using indirect memory references; in 
essence R1 and R2 are pointers.


> or
> indeed that it is the C code that puts the machine into the mode that
> allows access by real memory address, sans which it cannot be done.

Whether the C code is *allowed* permission to access a particular 
memory address or not has nothing to do with C, it has to do with 
whether or not the Operating System will allow the running application 
to do so.

Code running in Kernel mode is absolutely no different from code 
running in application mode; it merely has different permissions. Of 
course, kernel mode code can access all the memory in the machine it is 
running on (or in the virutal machine it is running on in the case of 
z/VM, VMWare, Parallels, etc.)

One of the jobs of an operating system is to manage memory...


> 
> 
> 
>> Just out of curiousity - are there any OS's written in Cobol?
>> I don't know of any, but I bet there are one or two out there. :)
```

```
On Dec 18, 4:32 pm, Paul <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote:
> On 2008-12-16 22:25:13 -0600, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> said:
>
…[83 more quoted lines elided]…
> by MacOS and UNIX.


> #include <stdio.h>
> int main(int argc, char *argv[]) {
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>   }

No, it is not the 'operating system' that causes the error, it is the
hardware. Your code is not attempting to access real address 100, it
is only attempting to access an address within the virtual machine
created for your program. Given that Intel is little-endian the
pointer has some segment reference that does not exist within that
virtual machine.

On a Z80 or 8086 a pointer to 0:100 would actually access the real
address 100, but that was a 30 years ago.

In protected mode the machine is _not_ a Z80 or 8086. You cannot
simply set a pointer to an arbitrary value and expect it to access
some data. Pointers are segment:offset and the only valid segments
that you can use without the hardware dumping you are the ones allowed
in your virtual machine.


> In Intel (or Wintel Parlance)
>
…[63 more quoted lines elided]…
> to do so.

No. Wrong. In protected mode it is the hardware that will prevent you
accessing arbitrary addresses. Even then these would be virtual
addresses and not the real address that you think you can get at.

And, has been pointed out before, it is the _implementation_ that
creates the code that runs in protected mode.


> Code running in Kernel mode is absolutely no different from code
> running in application mode; it merely has different permissions.

Actually the 'permissions' can allow special machine instructions to
be executed and, if the compiler can generate these then the code _is_
different from that running in application mode.



> Of
> course, kernel mode code can access all the memory in the machine it is
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

```
PR wrote:
> 
> Are you by some chance thinking that the hardware controls whether it
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> mode* is used.

Er - most modern CPUs with memory management do implement access rings
in hardware. The OS tells it when to switch (using privileged
instructions), of course, but access can be, and often is, enforced by
the CPU and MMU.

> By the way, under Windows, that *does* access physical memory address
> 100. Applications are ran in a flat memory model, and an absolute
> address, like the one I entered, actually does map to the same
> physical address.

Modern versions of Windows are paged virtual memory systems, and for
an ordinary process, there's no guarantee that any physical page is
mapped to the page slot that contains virtual memory address 100. (By
convention, Windows maps a page at the lowest virtual address, which
will include virtual address 100, but it doesn't *have* to.)

> You really don't understand how an OS works do you?  I recommend you
> go find an old copy of Tennnebaum's MINIX book.

Not terribly relevant for a modern OS. For that matter, even a book as
old as Bach's _The Design of the UNIX Operating System_ would correct
some of these misconceptions. (See the discussion of the page table in
6.2, pp 153-156.)
```

```
On Dec 19, 5:26 am, PR <paul.rauler...@gmail.com> wrote:
> On Dec 18, 12:46 am, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
>
…[190 more quoted lines elided]…
> mode* is used.

Certainly the OS is in control of what goes on. In many cases it is
the hardware that will do address translation and checking
permissions.The exception may well be passed back to the OS to deal
with but the OS is not usually involved with examining every CPU
cycle. (In fact that would require CPU cycles to do so, which it would
have to examine, which ...)


> By the way, under Windows, that *does* access physical memory address
> 100. Applications are ran in a flat memory model, and an absolute
> address, like the one I entered, actually does map to the same
> physical address.

You are claiming that the address translation tables do not do
anything.

If the program has legitimate access to a specific address then where
that lies in physical memory is determined by the ADT. In particular
two programs accessing what they think are the same address (ie
identical bit patterns in the pointer) will access completely
different physical memory locations because they have different ADTs.

So while your code may well try to access 'absolute address 100' where
that lies in _physical_ memory depends entirely on where the page
manager has loaded it for your process and the ADT will translate that
access to that page.


> The variable I defined, aCharVar, may be at *offset
> zero in the data segment*, but the virutal address will be wherever
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> No it isn't. It is the same code.

It is only "the same code" if the compiler is capable of generating
that code, and that depends on the particular _implementation_.

> Whether it MAY execute or not has
> nothing to do with
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> the application level code at a higher privilege level.

You may well be able to -in-assembler- but, getting back to the actual
topic, how do you get a particular C compiler to "write those
instructions into application level code" if there is no statement
that the compiler can use to tell it that.


>
> Indeed - what do you think system calls are?

In some systems 'system calls' are requests for the operating system
to perform some function on behalf of the user process. The system
call puts the request on one of the queues and suspends the process
(or not if it is async). The OS processes the request and then puts
the user process back to ready state so it can continue on some later
context switch.

Other systems may do it in other ways.


> > > Of
> > > course, kernel mode code can access all the memory in the machine it is
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

```
The point is you use service calls (or the equivalent) in an Operating System
to give application level code controlled access to protected 
instructions and capabilities.
```

```
On Dec 19, 3:30 pm, Paul <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote:

Which, however, is irrelevant to the general case.

Your calim was that: "an absolute address, like the one I entered,
actually does map to the same physical address".

Now you say that this only applies to a very small number of specific
addresses in some operating systems.

But, in fact, there is no assurance that the page containing those
small number of 'absolute address' is actually at that physical
address. That page _might_ be at any place in physical RAM and the ADT
will map it. In fact it may even be swapped out and be remapped to a
different physical address later.


> > If the program has legitimate access to a specific address then where
> > that lies in physical memory is determined by the ADT. In particular
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> read more »
```

```
On Dec 19, 4:35 pm, Paul <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote:
> On 2008-12-18 21:12:25 -0600, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> said:
>
…[263 more quoted lines elided]…
> You think computers run without software or something?

When the program attempts to execute an instruction that accesses an
arbitrary address, say, address 100 then on a real mode machine the
access will occur, on a protected mode machine where this address is
not allowed then it is the hardware (eg CPU/MMU/..) that detects that
the access is invalid.

The OS is not looking at every memory access made by the program and
determining whether it is allowed or not, the hardware is.

While the OS does create the ADT which forms the basis of what is
_allowed_ it is the hardware that uses these to detect when an access
is _outside_ those allowed and will prevent this.




> >> Of course we assume that an OS runs on a computer,
> >> and uses the facilities of said computer, be it CPU, MMU, or whatever els
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> read more »
```

```
On Dec 18, 8:30 pm, Paul <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote:
> I am not saying any such thing. I am saying that low memory is mapped
> to physical memory
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> used as examples, it DOES.


In Windows, at least the NT branch, virtual addresses 0x0 - 0x10000
are never mapped* to anything.  This helps catch null pointer
dereferences and similar errors.

Many *nix have a similar policy, although they vary greatly.  Linux,
for example reserves (and doesn't map*) a single page at the low end
of the address space on all architectures that I'm aware of.

It is, of course, quite common to have much/all of the kernal area
mapped into every address space.  In Linux and Windows, for example,
the high one or two GB (on 32 bit machines) are so mapped, but
typically with permissions that prevent the applications for accessing
them, except in limited ways.

Note that unlike zArch, there is *nothing* special about the first
page of memory, physical, virtual, or otherwise, in x86, at least once
you get out of real mode.


*It’s not impossible that in at least some versions those areas are
mapped to actual physical pages, but without and permissions that
would allow them to be accessed  - but that’s an internal detail for
the OS, to aid in tracking page table usage.  In fact I know of (a
different) OS that did just that.  Which doesn’t matter – the
application will still fault when it tries to access those pages.
```

```
On 2008-12-17 08:45:41 -0600, "Michael Mattias" <mmattias@talsystems.com> said:

LOL! Good point, and well taken.

I guess the reason is that there are things you simply cannot do in a 
computer unless you have access to the address of the data/code you are 
trying to manipulate. There are many more things you cannot do 
efficiently without that access.

And under the covers, all our compilers are basically doing is moving 
data around from place to place, memory to registers, memory to memory, 
etc. (Assuming of course, the machine has registers. :)

Even COBOL has the ADDRESS OF capability built into it, and COBOL is 
probably the least likely language to need address pointers I can think 
of. Well, outside of SmallTalk and a few others.

-Paul
```

```
On Dec 18, 7:37 am, "Michael Mattias" <mmatt...@talsystems.com> wrote:
> "Paul" <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote in message

…[38 more quoted lines elided]…
> mmatt...@talsystems.com

MMM- well- another author - that would be *me* has written for the
past 25 years that a program that gives the correct answers but takes
too long to do the job is worthless.

Also written, and taught, that programs that give correct answers, but
take an inordinate amount of resources to do so are not cost effective
in most business.

You can define resources anyway you want, it still comes out the same
way. If it takes too many programmer hours to implement or maintain,
it isn't worth it. Or too much DASD, or too high a license cost. You
*cannot* take a mono-centric viewpoint of "this is the way it has to
be" in terms of software, database, or network engineering, and you
*cannot* ignore the hardware that the software runs on. Not unless you
want to spend half your time justifying why the project does not work,
is over budget, or works but isn't fast enough.

And this is a very different discussion that is C Pointers point to
memory, which is where this started.

-Paul


As for the kinds of things I was talking about, perhaps you have never
written operating systems or operating system code. Think in terms of
task/thread/process control, file systems, indexed file systems,
network transmissions, communications over any kind of a data bus, and
so forth and so on.
```

```
> Z80 or a PDP-11, it may not be true for other machines.

The term "SYSTEM STORAGE" to a mainframer is equivalent to saying "RAM" 
to a PC person. It only takes on other meanings in the context of an OS 
or runtime environment.

Any kind of memory storage, by the way, has to be included at an 
address the machine can provide access to.

> 
> 
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
> execute access.

Technically, they are all just storage allocations - including the 
executable code.
I was speaking specifically of z/OS however, where the code section is 
not (usually) write protected.

I don't think I have ever encountered a system where the code section 
was not readable. Not sure how it would execute it if it could not read 
it.

Seen lots of cases where people put executable code in the data 
sections though. Annoying as that is.
```

```
PR wrote:
> 
> <amusement> Since the days of Von Neuman, executable code has been
> stored in addressable memory.

But not always.

> Do you have a real instance of a machine where the executable code in
> memory is not readable?

There are plenty of 8-bitters that cannot treat code as data - ie, it
can only be read for execution. And 8-bitters still represent
90-something percent of all computers sold in a given year.

DSPs are also usually Harvard-architecture machines (separate
instruction and data).

General-purpose computers are only a tiny segment of the overall
computer market. The most popular computer application in the world,
by far, is "digital clock", and you don't need a 64-bit processor for
that.
```

```
On 2008-12-18 12:01:59 -0600, Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com> said:

> PR wrote:
>> 
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> that.

I am not sure I understand what you are trying to say here. Usually, a 
CPU has a an instruction fetch unit that reads the instruction data 
from a memory address. That alone says to me that the instruction data 
space has to be readable. But perhaps you are talking about something 
entirely different.

A lot of machine have separate instruction and data spaces; when I say 
code or text section, I am talking about the instruction space. When I 
say data or static section, I am talking about the data space, or space 
that is read/write.

I know of no modern digital machines that are not stored program 
machines. Even most embedded processors qualify as GPDCs these days.

Do you have a specific instance of a machine like you are talking 
about? The closest examples I can thing of are some analog computers.
```

- **Message-ID:** `<ca8c7267-c9d1-4c0c-a5b5-a7cf7e79d2bd@s9g2000prg.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Dec 18, 8:44 pm, Paul <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote:
> I know of no modern digital machines that are not stored program
> machines. Even most embedded processors qualify as GPDCs these days.
>
> Do you have a specific instance of a machine like you are talking
> about? The closest examples I can thing of are some analog computers.



Consider the ubiquitous 8051 microcontroller.  Data memory and program
memory are physically totally separate.  And while there are
instructions that allow you to read from program memory (so you can
store lookup tables and whatnot in the program ROM), those
instructions are completely separate from the ones that you use to
access RAM.  Some versions of the 8051 provide a back door to write
the program ROM, but that's for things like downloading updated
firmware.

Many small controllers follow that model, and some even have different
word sizes for data and program memory.  Consider the PICs - of which
billions (literally) have been produced.  The PIC16s, for example,
have 8-bit wide RAM, and 14-bit wide program memory (other PIC
families vary - the PIC17s, for example having 16-bit program memory).

Higher end controllers do typically follow the more conventional Von
Neumann architecture in that both instructions and data are in a
single address space, rather than separate ones.
```

```
On Dec 19, 3:44 pm, Paul <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote:
> On 2008-12-18 12:01:59 -0600, Michael Wojcik <mwoj...@newsguy.com> said:
>
…[24 more quoted lines elided]…
> I am not sure I understand what you are trying to say here.

That is because you fail to follow the discussion but conflate and
inflate it in meaningless ways. You also generalise the specific and
specificise the general.

The discussion started started when a program could not write to
variables in its own code segment, but the claim was that it could
read these as constants.

I pointed out that with some systems a program may not even be able to
read its own code segment.

You then thought that this meant that _nothing_ could read it.

Do try and keep up.

> Usually, a
> CPU has a an instruction fetch unit that reads the instruction data
> from a memory address. That alone says to me that the instruction data
> space has to be readable. But perhaps you are talking about something
> entirely different.

But, in 'protected mode' the code segment may be _protected_ from
other processes _and_ even from its own process. That is any segment
(in a particular system) may be marked as 'not readable', 'read-only'
or 'read-write', but it may have another protection flag and and that
is 'executable' or 'non-execuatable'.

The 'instruction fetch unit' only cares about the 'executable' flag,
the _data_ fetch/put unit cares about the read/write flags.

How hard is this stuff ?

>
> A lot of machine have separate instruction and data spaces; when I say
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> about? The closest examples I can thing of are some analog computers.

Your knowledge is not a limitation on the types of machines that can
be built.

It is quite easy to imagine an 8bit machine where the instruction
fetch unit accesses, say, a 64Kb ROM and the data fetch/put unit
accesses a completely different 64Kb RAM.

That would mean that the only way of 'loading' a binary program is to
put it in a ROM on some other machine and swap it, but that is how
many embedded systems work anyway. Having the ROM and RAM in the same
address space would simply limit the total to 64Kb rather than each
having a full 64Kb available.
```
