```
On Dec 20, 11:32 am, PR <paul.rauler...@gmail.com> wrote:
> On Dec 18, 11:35 pm, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
>
…[92 more quoted lines elided]…
> control of the CPU when it is executing.

As I have already said, there is only one with a different perspective
in this discussion, and that is you.

While the instructions of a program may direct the CPU to operate in
particular ways 'the CPU' is _not_ 'the program'.

The program does not have to feed the next instruction into the CPU
(though it may specify where that is with a branch instruction) that
is the function of the 'instruction fetch' hardware.


> But unassuming clear logic like that will confuse you I bet.

What does 'confuse' is the way that you conflate 'program' and 'CPU'
as if they are the same thing.

"""Which is a silly statement. Of course it [the program] has to read
its own code segment, otherwise it cannot execute it."""

That statement of yours is nonsense, the CPU is _not_ the program. The
CPU can still execute the code even if the program is prevented from
executing an instruction that copies one of those instructions into a
data variable (for example).


In fact you seem so stuck in your perspective that 'the program' and
'the CPU' are the same thing that the examples seem to make no
impression on you.


> So let me
> cover some of the unrelated and silly things you are liable to bring
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> CPU.

'Control of the CPU' (using your terms) is _not_ 'being the CPU' as
your statements seem claim.

A driver may have control of the engine in a car but that doesn't mean
that he has to be able to suck the petrol from the gas tank.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: C and Low-level Storage

_(reply depth: 20)_

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-12-18T22:11:29-08:00

```
'Other folks' have explained a _completely_ different mechanism which
is why it is 'different from what I said'. Perhaps you didn't notice
that a 8051 is not an ICL 2900.

Two separate and distinct mechanisms, one for the 2900 series, the
other in 8051 and other micro devices. Why do you conflate and confuse
these ?

They both prevent a [user] program having read access to their code
segments in completely different ways, and yet the computer can
execute the code in both.
```

```
Richard wrote:
> On Dec 20, 11:27 am, PR <paul.rauler...@gmail.com> wrote:
>> On Dec 19, 12:11 am, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>>>>>> <mwoj...@newsguy.com> said:
<snip>

>> You only call it 'nonsense' because you fail to understand it.
>>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> having difficulty.

As an observer of this discussion, I have to say that I don't believe you 
have "twisted" anything. Your analogy of the  car driver not having to suck 
the petrol was a very good one.

And yet, I would ask you this Richard:  Do you honestly believe that Paul 
doesn't know or understand how a program works?

The idea of a program doing instruction fetches is not so far from the truth 
(even though it is not exactly what happens) because if there were no 
program there would be no need for instruction fetching.  However, if we 
substitute "causing" for "doing" in the previous sentence the meaning 
becomes clearer.

On the other hand, if you intend to discuss the mechanics of how 
instructions get fetched and placed in an ALU, it is probably necessary to 
agree that level of discussion before assuming it.

The difficulties here seem to be in the exactitude of  the terminology and 
how it is used. Is there such a wide divide between the following?:

1. "The driver causes the engine to consume fuel". (It's certainly true that 
the engine won't consume fuel unless the driver switches it on...)
2. "The driver causes fuel to be delivered to the engine."  (We know it is 
the fuel pump that does this, but that is implicit in the statement. The 
fuel pump won't be activated unless the engine is switched on.)
3. "When the driver starts the engine, fuel is delivered to it via a 
carburettor or fuel injection system, fed by a fuel pump."

The fact that we may prefer the third statement, does not make the other two 
"wrong".

 I believe Paul feels you are twisting what he says because he is saying 
things that he expects to be interpreted in a normal way and not 
pedantically to the finest level of detail.

You like exactness and interpret his statements in a much tighter way than 
he intended. That isn't wrong, but it isn't necessarily good communication 
either.

(Because of the exact nature of computer programming, most of us here have a 
tendency to do this. It frequently generates heat in this forum. None of us, 
even the ones like me who are very careful about the use of words, can be 
absolutely exact all of the time. We all say things which other people 
interpret differently than we intended, and get miffed because it seems that 
people are deliberately twisting what we say. I believe the Americanism is: 
"Hey, cut me some slack here..." ... which may be construed by some as an 
apology for inaccuracy... )

That's what prompted my question to you. If you stand back and look at the 
discussion you will find two very competent and experienced people who 
certainly know what they are talking about, arguing to the point of 
hostility about things which both of them fully understand.

Two eagles fighting for a cockroach.

I don't believe Paul needs an exposition on the difference between code 
protection and execution protection, (any more than I needed one on 
Relational Databases recently... :-)),  and I don't honestly believe you 
would think that necessary if you stopped and looked at who you are 
responding to. Spelling it out for a Newbie is good; doing it for someone 
with decades of experience is unnecessary and simply creates antagonism.

The sad thing here is that the two of you will probably resent each other 
for some time to come because of the tone of the exchanges, when in fact, if 
you were both working on a project together you'd  probably afford each 
other respect and get along famously.

Respect.

Pete.
```

```
On Dec 20, 2:03 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> Richard wrote:
> > On Dec 20, 11:27 am, PR <paul.rauler...@gmail.com> wrote:
…[29 more quoted lines elided]…
> doesn't know or understand how a program works?

I think that he understands far too well. I can visualise him over an
assembler listing pointing his pencil at each line in turn and
actualising the bit patterns in his brain.

I think that he does consider the program and his mental processing of
the bit patterns as the same thing. I think that he is is so ingrained
with this image that he completely fails to be able to separate these
into any other perspective.


> The idea of a program doing instruction fetches is not so far from the truth
> (even though it is not exactly what happens) because if there were no
> program there would be no need for instruction fetching.

Interestingly some processors do 'run' without there being a program
in memory, it is just that nothing useful results. With a completely
zero memory, the fetches are done, the 'instruction' is decoded and
executed, the result depends on the particular CPU.


> However, if we
> substitute "causing" for "doing" in the previous sentence the meaning
> becomes clearer.

I disagree with either statement. The CPU does fetches without 'the
program' doing or causing _anything_ there is no 'get next
instruction' in the program, that is entirely in the CPU. There is no
'increment the instruction pointer', granted the program does contain
'set instruction pointer to ..' instructions.

In fact, in many cases, the program has much less control than
claimed. When a hardware interrupt occurs, such as a clock tick, then
there is no 'program' that causes the instruction fetch switch to some
other location.

As it happens there may well be systems, process control perhaps,
where a hardware interrupt does not cause a switch, but merely sets a
flag that 'the program' will detect on tits next state cycle.

It may well be that is what Paul considers, and it may be that is
_all_ he knows of.


> On the other hand, if you intend to discuss the mechanics of how
> instructions get fetched and placed in an ALU, it is probably necessary to
> agree that level of discussion before assuming it.

Paul would probly still claim IO was twisting things.

> The difficulties here seem to be in the exactitude of  the terminology and
> how it is used. Is there such a wide divide between the following?:
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> "wrong".

Maybe, but Paul was saying the equivalent of 'engine won't run if the
driver can't suck up the fuel'.


>  I believe Paul feels you are twisting what he says because he is saying
> things that he expects to be interpreted in a normal way and not
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> either.

'Poor communication' is when the discussion is described as 'C
accessing memory' when it is about a much smaller subset of that.
Anyone can 'win' by misrepresenting what the discussion is about.


> (Because of the exact nature of computer programming, most of us here have a
> tendency to do this. It frequently generates heat in this forum. None of us,
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> protection and execution protection,

And yet he called the concept of it existing 'silly'.


> (any more than I needed one on
> Relational Databases recently... :-)),  and I don't honestly believe you
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> other respect and get along famously.

You think ???   ;-)

> "I used to write COBOL...now I can do anything."
```

```
which might be of relevance here:

'You age.  As you age you'll find that there are only two things you need 
to know:

1) A man must know his limits.  The Craftsman knows that he is not an 
Artist, the Artist knows he is not a Wise One, the Wise One knows... that 
he does not know.

2) A man must work for improvement, for himself and for others.  The 
Craftsman works so that some day he may be an Artist, the Artist works so 
that some day he will be a Wise One and the Wise One works with other Wise 
Ones, knowing that the way you keep a piece of metal sharp is by, at 
times, rubbing it against a harder piece of metal.

3) There are *always* more than two things to know.'

A little lengthy for the average fortune-cookie, I know... maybe there's a 
market for multi-layer fortune cakes?

DD
```

```
On 2008-12-19 00:11:29 -0600, Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> said:

> On Dec 19, 5:51ï¿½pm, Paul <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote:
>> On 2008-12-18 21:45:45 -0600, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> said:
…[54 more quoted lines elided]…
> again, it was 'low-level access' or 'access to system memory'.

No- it was whether or noe C pointers are always pointers to memory. The 
distiniction of "low level access" only makes a difference from a high 
level language that supports objects or some other higher level 
abstraction.

Access to "system memory" is different from access to "system storage", 
and the term can change in the context you use it.

However, it makes no difference. C can and does access any memory 
exactly the same way. If there is something there to deny access to 
that memory, it is not an inherent property of the C language, it is a 
property of the system the C program is running on.

Again, I doubt you will be able to understand that, as your entire 
agenda appears to not be rational discourse, but rather to prove to 
everyone how much of an ass you can be.

Congratulations by the way, you are doing a good job of doing that.

Why am I am even bothering to post this? Sigh - never mind. I quit. 
I'll go back to lurking around for another couple years or so.

-Paul
```

```
On Dec 19, 5:28 am, PR <paul.rauler...@gmail.com> wrote:
> On Dec 18, 1:08 am, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
>
…[124 more quoted lines elided]…
> to be addressable.

You appear to conflate various terms and also to inflate the scope of
an argument beyond its actual bounds.

The issue was whether, on a particular system, a user program had read
access to its own code. Of course it is always readable by the
hardware, of course it is always 'addressable' by _something_.


> Do you have a real instance of a machine where the executable code in
> memory is not readable?

Yes I do: the ICL 2900 Series. "A code segment can be marked as
'execute only' and can be protected from accidental writing or reading
within the process. Data segments can be marked as 'data' (to prevent
accidental execution) and as either 'read only' or 'read and write'."

"The ICL 2900 Series", J.K.Buckle. 1978.
```

```
On Dec 19, 3:57 pm, Paul <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote:

>
…[171 more quoted lines elided]…
> other words?

Your statement "That memory doesn't have to be writable - as in the
case of ROM memory, but it does have to be addressable." appeared to
be contradicting the statement that executable code only needs to be
readable by the CPU (and not the program).

If you weren't conflating 'readable' and 'addressable' then why did
you make that statement which otherwise seemed obvious and
unnecessary ?



> >> Do you have a real instance of a machine where the executable code in
> >> memory is not readable?
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> Do you have any information on how this worked?

Well, yes, I obviously have "The ICL 2900 Series", J.K.Buckle. 1978.
Did you not notice that ? I do have many other papers on ICL machines
too, including others on 2900.

> I confess, I cannot
> imagine how a computer could deal with execute only access to a stored
> program.

Perhaps you failed to read where it said "within the process". As I
said before, you generalise from the specific. The specific case was
'a program reading constants from the code segment', you then seem to
generalise this to 'a (the whole) computer'.

Perhaps it isn't obvious to you that 'the program' which is prevented
from reading its own code segment is only a part of 'a computer', and
that other parts can have different access permissions. For example
the demand page unit _can_ read and write code segments and this unit
isn't "within the program".


> Branching alone would seem to present a challenge, never mind
> things like instruction pipelining and so forth.

Why ?  What is difficult to understand about this ? Why would
_a_program_ need to read its own code segment ?

Just so you don't get confused again and to clarify;

Why would a program need to do: 'MOVE xyz TO some_variable', where xyz
was in the code segment, in order to do a branch ?


> Is this a modern machine?

You should have got a clue from the '1978' as the publication date. It
was however _very_ modern and advanced for its time.

> The only time I remember hearing much about
> ICL anything was I *think* as the GPDC installed about Brit Nimrod ASW
> planes. But I thought that had an IBM 360 instruction set, and that was
> three decades ago.

The ICL System 4 (inherited from English Electric-Leo-Marconi) was a
licence built Spectra 70 and so was IBM 360ish. This pre-dated the
2900 series by a decade or so.
```

```
On Dec 17, 9:38 pm, Paul <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote:
> I don't think I have ever encountered a system where the code section
> was not readable. Not sure how it would execute it if it could not read
> it.


For machines that support non-readable/executable segments, it's just
that the instruction fetch mechanism has different permissions than
the data read mechanism.  It's not different in principal than the
many systems that support (and use) non-executable data sections -
although then the reverse applies - it's the instruction fetch
mechanism that doesn't have permission to read the area of memory.
```

- **References:** `<494095a5$0$5502$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <4941e558$0$5493$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <ghtno4$hvh$1@reader1.panix.com> <ghufmm41br2@news1.newsguy.com> <ghv3ut$2vh$3@reader1.panix.com> <gi63hm324ng@news7.newsguy.com> <gi6ijn$a0h$1@reader1.panix.com> <e0kdk4pq6b46660uq2b7shvoc7lrs3ljm4@4ax.com> <gi6slm$1ml$1@reader1.panix.com> <gi94kc2qt9@news2.newsguy.com> <esW1l.439764$5p1.422312@en-nntp-06.dc1.easynews.com> <494874ee$0$5502$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net>`

```
Paul wrote:
> 
> Technically, C can access any address the machine can provide access to,
> including any system storage.

There's no requirement that a C implementation use the native address
type of the underlying machine for its pointers. There are many cases
where it does not.

A 32-bit C implementation running on a 64-bit system can't "access any
address the machine can provide access to". There are many such
implementations, since most of the general-purpose 64-bit machines
still support 32-bit C programming. There are various ones for
Windows, assorted Unixes, etc.

On the original RIOS RS/6000s (which I worked on before and after they
went GA, when I was with IBM), the machine address space was 56-bit,
but C (and all other languages, including machine code) used a 32-bit
address type. The additional bits were provided by segmentation via
the MMU. Privileged code (ie, the kernel) could effectively address
the entire address space by manipulating segments, but ordinary
processes couldn't, and none of them had a primitive type that
represented it.

And so on.
```

```
William M. Klein wrote:
>   I can't tell.  Have you been debating a "real-world" issue or a philosophical 
> one?
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> program" to system-level storage?

Yes. I mentioned some in the post you're following up on.

> I am NOT a C programmer, but when I have interacted with those that do use it, 
> it seems to me that all implementations that they use DO allow the C program to 
> access (at least "read") data at "system controlled" (low-level?) storage.

I'm not sure what that definition would cover. A great many C programs
execute in virtual storage and cannot read data at arbitrary locations.

> If there are C implementations that do not allow programmers to "read" the 
> storage where "system control blocks, etc" reside, I would be interested in 
> which C and on what platforms these exist.

Pretty much any C program written for a hosted implementation on a
virtual-memory system. That includes most modern general-purpose
computers.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: I need a new compiler...

_(reply depth: 7)_

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2008-12-16T15:41:52-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gi94kb1qt9@news2.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<gi6ijn$a0h$1@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<494095a5$0$5502$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <ghufmm41br2@news1.newsguy.com> <ghv3ut$2vh$3@reader1.panix.com> <gi63hm324ng@news7.newsguy.com> <gi6ijn$a0h$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
> In article <gi63hm324ng@news7.newsguy.com>,
> Michael Wojcik  <mwojcik@newsguy.com> wrote:
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> --end quoted text

Note it says nothing about any correspondence between such a reference
and "low-level memory". As defined by the specification of the C
language, it is a reference to a location in the virtual machine. In a
particular C implementation, it might be a reference to a physical
memory location (as in many small systems), or to a location in
process virtual memory (as in many general-purpose systems), or to an
object in system-wide virtual store (as in the capability architecture
used by IBM's iSeries, formerly AS/400, line).

Or it could be a magic token that summons an invisible fairy who
fetches the data from a mushroom in the forest. The definition of the
language *leaves this up to the implementation*.

> Now... if the reference provided by a pointer is one towards low-level 
> memory (a sub-set of 'anywhere')

If it is. But it may not be, since there are many other subsets of
"anywhere". The quoted text doesn't say that pointers could be
constructed to point to anywhere; it says the object referred to by
the pointer could be anywhere.
```

```
I should have added that in the late 60's the "Journal of the ACM" had an 
article that said something to the effect that all processing could be 
driven by thorough description of data structures.  That's why I like COBOL 
so much.  By describing data structures, the code needed to deal with 
arithmetic or logic can ignore these details.  The programmer is unlikely to 
scribble on memory outside of his data division.  No wonder more lines of 
COBOL code (a quarter of a trillion lines?) are in use today, with billions 
more written every year.  And consider the number of lines of lower level 
language that are needed to do what single lines of COBOL do.  "string" and 
"unstring delimited by" are good examples.  I dread the flaming responses 
that these comments will cause.  Please, just let me express my opinion 
without being punished.  Paul
```

```
Paul H wrote:

> had an article that said something to the effect that all processing
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> year.

Not intended as "punishment" (believe whatever you want to...) but:

1. The estimate of 5 billion lines of COBOL a year is suspect, but even if 
it were true, the lines written in Java alone are around 5 times that. (To 
be fair, these estimates are just as suspect as the COBOL ones... no-one 
REALLY knows.)

2. The quarter of a trillion lines of COBOL came from a Gartner report (as 
Gartner do business with a large COBOL user base it can hardly be seen as 
unbiased...) that is now over 10 years old. In that time billions of lines 
of that codebase have been eroded by conversion, replacement, and 
retirement, and this process proceeds and accellerates every year.

The point is that it doesn't matter how many LOC are written in ANY 
language; what matters is the cost of writing it and whether it can be or 
needs to be, maintained.

> And consider the number of lines of lower level language that
> are needed to do what single lines of COBOL do.  "string" and
> "unstring delimited by" are good examples.  I dread the flaming

> express my opinion without being punished.  Paul

There are a number of powerful constructs in COBOL and these are just two of 
them. COBOL's problems were never with the power of the language.

This is not a "flaming" response, Paul, just civilized discussion and 
observation... :-)

Pete.
```

```
>  And consider the number of lines of lower level language that
> are needed to do what single lines of COBOL do.  "string" and
> "unstring delimited by" are good examples.  I dread the flaming
> responses that these comments will cause.  Please, just let me
> express my opinion without being punished.  Paul

A better example would be floating the currency symbol to the immediate left 
of the most significant number or check-protecting a field. Trivial.

Conversely, computing the hyperbolic arc tangent is pretty tough in COBOL 
(I've only done it once) - but just one statement in FORTRAN.
```

```
Funny thing Paul, I was looking at Clarion the other day. Found (to my 
astonishment) that it supports ActiveX and COM components. This makes it a 
very powerful tool. You could get or build components to do what you want 
and "glue" them together with Clarion... RealBasic may also be a good 
option. (Visual Basic is free from MicroSoft and you can develop it in 
Visual Studio Express, which is also free...)

As for database...I can personally recommend MySQL which is free. MS Access 
(2000 and above) has also come a long way from its humble (and generally 
poorly implemented) beginnings.
```
